Forgiveness of Non-Kufr Sins: Rational and Textual Permission
The author explains that forgiveness of non-kufr sins is possible rationally and textually.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why forgiveness of sins other than kufr is permissible both rationally and textually, and how this relates to intercession.

Core Idea
Kufr is textually unforgivable, but all other sins may be forgiven.

This lesson examines a key point in Islamic theology: the scope of divine forgiveness. The author argues that while kufr (disbelief) is textually impossible to forgive, other sins can be forgiven both rationally and textually. This establishes the basis for intercession and the proper treatment of believers who sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's chain of reasoning come through.
Notice the poetic line and the commentary that follows.
2
Identify the two types of permissibility
The passage distinguishes between rational (aqlan) and textual (sam'an) permissibility. Find where each is mentioned and how they apply to kufr and non-kufr.
The verse from Surah al-Nisa' (4:48) is the textual evidence.
3
Reflect on the wisdom and critique
Consider the wisdom given for why sins are forgivable but not kufr. Then note the author's critique that this reasoning is deficient because intercession is broader.
The author says 'fihi qusur' – there is deficiency – and invites reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذْ جَائِزٌ غُفْرانُ غَيْرِ الكُفْرِ فَلَا نُكَفِّرْ مُؤْمِناً بِالوِزْرِ) قوله : (إِذْ  َجائِزٌ غُفْرانُ غَيْرِ الكُفْرِ) هذا تعليل للشفاعة، فكأنه قال: لأنه يجوز عقلاً وسمعاً غفران غير الكفر من الذنوب بلا شفاعة، فبالشفاعة أولى، وأما غفران الكفر ،فهو، وإن جاز عقلاً، ممتنع سمعاً ؛ قال تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن بشرك به وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ) [النساء: ٤٨] ، وعُلم مما تقرر أن المراد بالجواز في كلام المصنف الجواز العقلي والسمعي معاً، ولذلك قيد بغير الكفر؛ لأن غفران الكفر ممتنع سمعاً، وإن جاز عقلاً. والحكمة في غفران الذنوب دون الكفر أنها لا تنفك عن خوف عقاب ورجاء عفو ورحمة بخلاف الكفر ، وذلك أن صاحب الذنوب مُسلم يعتقد نقص نفسه، فيخاف العقاب، ويرجو العفو والرحمة بخلافِ صاحبِ الكفر فإنه لا يعتقد نقص نفسه، فلا يخاف العقاب، ولا يرجو العفو والرحمة ولا يخفى أن هذا التعليل الذي ذكره المصنف فيه قصور؛ لأنَّ الشفاعة شاملة في فصل القضاء(۱)، وللشفاعة في غفران الذنوب، وهذا التعليل خاص بالشفاعة في غفران الذنوب، فتأمله .
Since forgiveness of other than kufr is permissible, so we do not declare a believer a disbeliever for sin. His statement: (Since forgiveness of other than kufr is permissible) this is a reason for intercession, as if he said: Because it is rationally and textually permissible to forgive sins other than kufr without intercession, then by intercession it is more fitting. As for forgiveness of kufr, although it is rationally permissible, it is textually impossible; He, exalted, said: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills.' And it is known from what has been established that what is meant by permissibility in the author's statement is both rational and textual permissibility, and therefore he restricted it to other than kufr; because forgiveness of kufr is textually impossible, although rational. And the wisdom in forgiving sins but not kufr is that they (sins) are not free from fear of punishment and hope of pardon and mercy, unlike kufr. That is because the possessor of sins is a Muslim who believes in the deficiency of his soul, so he fears punishment and hopes for pardon and mercy, unlike the possessor of kufr, for he does not believe in the deficiency of his soul, so he does not fear punishment nor hope for pardon and mercy. And it is not hidden that this reasoning which the author mentioned has deficiency; because intercession is comprehensive in the chapter of judgment, and intercession is for forgiveness of sins, and this reasoning is specific to intercession for forgiveness of sins, so ponder it.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage begins by quoting the poem line 'Since forgiveness of other than kufr is permissible, so we do not declare a believer a disbeliever for sin.' The author explains that this is a justification (ta'lil) for intercession. It argues that since it is permissible both rationally (aqlan) and textually (sam'an) to forgive sins other than kufr without intercession, then intercession is even more fitting. As for kufr, although rational permissibility exists, textual impossibility stands due to the verse from Surah al-Nisa' (4:48). Thus, the author's use of 'permissible' (ja'iz) encompasses both rational and textual permissibility, which is why he restricted it to non-kufr. Then the author provides the wisdom (hikmah) behind forgiving sins but not kufr: the sinner is a Muslim who acknowledges his soul's deficiency, leading to fear of punishment and hope for pardon. The disbeliever, lacking this belief, has no such fear or hope. However, the author notes that this reasoning is deficient because intercession applies broadly to the entire judgment (fasl al-qada), not just to forgiveness of sins, and the reasoning offered by al-shanuwri? (the author of the poem) is specific to the latter. The reader is encouraged to reflect (tamall) on this.

Key Terms
غفران
جواز عقلي
جواز سمعي
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that kufr is impossible both rationally and textually to forgive.
Correction: The passage clarifies that forgiveness of kufr is only textually impossible, but rationally permissible.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'ja'iz' (permissible) in the author's statement according to the passage?
A) Rational permission only
B) Textual permission only
C) Both rational and textual permission
D) Neither, it is impossible

Key Takeaway
Forgiveness of sins other than kufr is permissible both rationally and textually, while kufr is only textually impossible to forgive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What sins in your life have you dismissed as 'too small' while this passage reminds you that every non-kufr sin is serious yet forgivable? Will you start taking them more seriously and turning to Allah in repentance?
?
The passage says the Muslim sinner fears punishment and hopes for mercy. Honestly examine your heart: Do you truly fear Allah's punishment for your sins, or have you become complacent expecting automatic forgiveness? How will you revive that fear and hope today?
?
The author's reasoning is criticized as deficient, reminding you to think deeply about religious texts. Are you willing to challenge your own understanding and seek deeper answers, even when the initial explanation seems comfortable? What step will you take this week to wrestle with a difficult aqeedah concept?

Ruling on Not Excommunicating a Believer for Sin
The author states the rule against excommunication for sin and its two exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that believers are not declared disbelievers for committing sins, unless the sin itself is a cause of disbelief (mukaaffir) or the person deems it lawful (istihlal).

Core Idea
Sins do not expel a believer from faith unless the sin is disbelief or the sinner deems it lawful.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid clarifies a central Ash'ari principle: committing a sin, even a major one, does not remove a person from the fold of Islam. However, there are two crucial exceptions that protect the boundaries of faith. Let's read the author's precise conditions and understand the balance between mercy and doctrinal integrity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the conditional phrasing (بشرط) that introduces the exceptions.
2
Identify the two conditions for excommunication
Find the two conditions under which a believer would be declared a disbeliever. The first relates to the nature of the sin, the second to the sinner's attitude.
The first condition is about the sin being a 'mukaaffir', the second about 'istihlal'.
3
Understand 'mukaaffirat' and 'istihlal'
Learn the meaning of these key terms: mukaaffirat are sins that inherently constitute disbelief (e.g., denying Allah's knowledge), while istihlal means considering a known forbidden act as permissible.
Both are distinct from ordinary sins, which do not remove faith.
4
Reflect on the balance of the ruling
Think about why the Ash'aris make this distinction: it protects believers from being declared disbelievers for ordinary sins while preserving the seriousness of disbelief.
This ruling is a middle path between Kharijite extremism and Murji'ite laxity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فَلَا نُكَفِّرْ مُؤْمِناً بِالوِزْرِ مُفرَّعٌ على ما ذُكر ؛ أي: فلا نكفر، بالنون؛ أي: معاشر أهل السنة أو بالتاء؛ أي: أيها المخاطب أحداً من المؤمنين بارتكاب الذنب صغيرة كان الذنبُ أو كبيرة عالماً كان مرتكبه أو جاهلاً، بشرط ألا يكون ذلك الذنب من المكفرات كإنكار علمه تعالى بالجزئيات، وإلا كفر مرتكبه قطعاً، وبشرط ألا يكون مُستحلا له (۲) ؛ وهو معلوم من الدين بالضرورة كالزنا، وإلا كفر باستحلاله لذلك (٣)
His statement: (So we do not declare a believer a disbeliever for sin) is derived from what was mentioned; meaning: we do not declare, with the nun (meaning: we, the community of Ahl al-Sunnah) or with the ta (meaning: O addressee) anyone of the believers a disbeliever due to committing a sin, whether the sin is minor or major, whether the committer is knowledgeable or ignorant, on condition that this sin is not among the mukaaffirat (things that necessitate disbelief) such as denying His knowledge of particulars—for then its committer is definitely a disbeliever—and on condition that he does not consider it halal (permissible); and that (prohibition) is known from the religion by necessity, like adultery; otherwise he becomes a disbeliever due to considering it halal.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage explains the poem line 'So we do not declare a believer a disbeliever for sin' (fala nukaffir mu'minan bil-wizr). The author notes that this is a corollary (mufarra') of the previous discussion on forgiveness. The ruling is that we (Ahl al-Sunnah) do not excommunicate any believer for committing a sin, whether the sin is minor or major, and whether the committer is knowledgeable or ignorant. However, two conditions are set: First, the sin must not be among the mukaaffirat (things that entail disbelief), such as denying Allah's knowledge of particulars; if it is, then the person is definitely deemed a disbeliever. Second, the person must not consider the sin halal (permissible); the prohibition of such a sin must be known from the religion by necessity (e.g., adultery). If he considers it halal despite clear textual prohibition, then he becomes a disbeliever due to istihlal (deeming permissible what is known to be forbidden). This is a standard Ash'ari/Maturidi position that distinguishes sinful believers from disbelievers.

Key Terms
تكفير
المكفرات
استحلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any sin, especially major ones, automatically makes a believer a disbeliever.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only specific sins (mukaaffirat) or deeming a forbidden act permissible (istihlal) cause excommunication; ordinary sins do not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one condition that would cause a believer to be declared a disbeliever for a sin?
A) The sin is a major sin
B) The sinner is ignorant
C) The sinner considers the sin halal
D) The sin is repeated

Key Takeaway
A believer is not excommunicated for any sin unless it is a mukaaffir or he deems it halal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever privately doubted the faith of a fellow Muslim because of a sin they committed? How does this passage challenge your inner judgment and call you to correct that attitude—starting today?
?
Is there any sin you have committed that you have mentally justified or minimized, perhaps thinking 'it's not that bad'? Could that be crossing into istihlal? What concrete step will you take to repent and restore your awareness of its gravity?
?
The passage says we do not excommunicate even an ignorant sinner. Does your heart truly accept that a Muslim who commits a major sin like zina remains a believer? Or do you find yourself wishing they were 'out of the fold'? Repent of that wish, and ask Allah to purify your heart from takfiri tendencies.

The Permissibility of Forgiveness for Sins Other Than Disbelief
Explanation of forgiveness for non-disbelief sins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why forgiveness for sins other than disbelief is rationally and scripturally permissible, and how this principle underlies the doctrine of intercession.

Core Idea
Forgiveness of non-disbelief sins is possible; disbelief is unforgivable by revelation.

This passage establishes that Allah can forgive any sin except disbelief, both rationally and according to revelation. The commentator explains that intercession is more appropriate for these sins, and provides a wisdom: the sinner hopes for mercy, unlike the disbeliever. A critique of the reasoning is also noted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how the commentator links the permissibility of forgiveness to intercession.
Key verse: Quran 4:48.
2
Understand the distinction
Distinguish between rational permissibility and scriptural prohibition. Why is forgiveness of disbelief blocked scripturally?
3
Reflect on the wisdom
Consider the psychological difference between a sinner (fear and hope) and a disbeliever (no sense of deficiency). How does this explain the distinction?
4
Note the critique
The commentator finds the author's reasoning incomplete because intercession covers more than just forgiveness of sins. Ponder this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إِذْ جائِزٌ غُفْرانُ غَيْرِ الكُفْرِ) هذا تعليل للشفاعة، فكأنه قال: لأنه يجوز عقلاً وسمعاً غفران غير الكفر من الذنوب بلا شفاعة، فبالشفاعة أولى، وأما غفران الكفر، فهو، وإن جاز عقلاً، ممتنع سمعاً ؛ قال تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يشرك به وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ﴾ [النساء: ٤٨]، وعُلم مما تقرر أن المراد بالجواز في كلام المصنف الجواز العقلي والسمعي معاً، ولذلك قيد بغير الكفر؛ لأن غفران الكفر ممتنع سمعاً، وإن جاز عقلاً. والحكمة في غفران الذنوب دون الكفر أنها لا تنفك عن خوف عقاب ورجاء عفو ورحمة بخلاف الكفر، وذلك أن صاحب الذنوب مسلم يعتقد نقص نفسه، فيخاف العقاب، ويرجو العفو والرحمة بخلاف صاحب الكفر فإنه لا يعتقد نقص نفسه، فلا يخاف العقاب، ولا يرجو العفو والرحمة. ولا يخفى أن هذا التعليل الذي ذكره المصنف فيه قصور؛ لأن الشفاعة شاملة في فصل القضاء، وللشفاعة في غفران الذنوب، وهذا التعليل خاص للشفاعة في غفران الذنوب، فتأمله. قوله : (فَلَا نُكَفِّرْ مُؤْمِناً بِالوِزْرِ) مفرع على ما ذكر؛ أي: فلا نكفر، بالنون؛ أي: معاشر أهل السنة أو بالتاء؛ أي: أيها المخاطب أحداً من المؤمنين بارتكاب الذنب صغيرة كان الذنب أو كبيرة عالماً كان مرتكبه أو جاهلاً، بشرط ألا يكون ذلك الذنب من المكفرات كإنكار علمه تعالى بالجزئيات، وإلا كفر مرتكبه قطعاً، وبشرط ألا يكون مستحلاً له (۲)؛ وهو معلوم من الدين بالضرورة كالزنا، وإلا كفر باستحلاله لذلك (۳). قال العلامة ابن عابدين في حاشيته (۱۱/۵): (وأنت خبير بأن الصحيح في المعتزلة والرافضة وغيرهم من المبتدعة أنه لا يحكم بكفرهم وإن سبوا الصحابة أو استحلوا قتلنا بشبهة دليل، كالخوارج الذين استحلوا قتل الصحابة). وخالفت الخوارج فكفروا مرتكب الذنوب وجعلوا جميع الذنوب كبائر، كما سيأتي، ولم يكفروا بتكفير مرتكب الذنوب، مع أن من كفر مؤمناً كفر؛ لأنهم قالوا ذلك بتأويل واجتهاد. وأما المعتزلة فأخرجوا مرتكب الكبيرة من الإيمان، ولم يدخلوه في الكفر إلا بالاستحلال، فجعلوه منزلة بين المنزلتين، فمرتكب الكبيرة مخلد عند الفريقين في النار، ويُعذَّب عند الخوارج عذاب الكفار، وعند المعتزلة عذاب الفساق.
His statement: 'Since it is permissible to forgive other than disbelief' is a justification for intercession, so it is as if he said: Because it is permissible rationally and scripturally to forgive sins other than disbelief without intercession, so with intercession it is more appropriate. As for forgiveness of disbelief, although rationally permissible, it is scripturally prohibited; Allah said: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills.' [An-Nisa 48]. It is understood from what has been established that what is meant by permissibility in the author's speech is both rational and scriptural permissibility, therefore he restricted it to other than disbelief because forgiveness of disbelief is scripturally prohibited, even if rationally permissible. The wisdom in forgiving sins without disbelief is that they are not free from fear of punishment and hope of pardon and mercy, unlike disbelief. That is because the sinner is a Muslim who believes his soul is deficient, so he fears punishment and hopes for pardon and mercy, unlike the disbeliever who does not believe his soul is deficient, so he does not fear punishment nor hope for pardon and mercy. It is not hidden that this reasoning mentioned by the author is deficient because intercession is comprehensive in the chapter of judgment, and for intercession in forgiveness of sins, and this reasoning is specific to intercession in forgiveness of sins, so ponder it. His statement: 'So we do not excommunicate a believer for sin' is a branch of what was mentioned: i.e., we do not excommunicate, with the nun (i.e., we the community of Ahl al-Sunnah) or with the ta (i.e., you the addressee) anyone among the believers for committing a sin, whether the sin is minor or major, whether the committer is knowledgeable or ignorant, on condition that that sin is not among the excommunicating matters such as denying His knowledge of particulars, otherwise its committer is excommunicated absolutely, and on condition that he does not deem it permissible (2); that is known from the religion by necessity like adultery, otherwise he is excommunicated for deeming it permissible (3). Al-Allamah Ibn Abidin said in his gloss (11/5): 'You are well aware that the correct position regarding the Mu'tazila, the Rafidah, and other innovators is that they are not judged to be disbelievers even if they insult the Companions or deem it permissible to kill us due to a semblance of evidence, like the Khawarij who deemed it permissible to kill the Companions.' The Khawarij opposed and excommunicated the committer of sins and made all sins major, as will come, and they were not excommunicated for excommunicating the committer of sins, even though whoever excommunicates a believer becomes a disbeliever, because they said that with interpretation and ijtihad. As for the Mu'tazila, they removed the committer of a major sin from faith, and did not enter him into disbelief except with deemed permissibility, so they made it a station between two stations. Thus the committer of a major sin is eternally in Hell according to both groups, and is punished with the punishment of disbelievers according to the Khawarij, and with the punishment of sinners according to the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two lines of the poem. It begins by explaining the line 'Since it is permissible to forgive other than disbelief.' The commentator states that this is a justification for intercession (shafa'a): both reason and revelation show that forgiveness of sins other than disbelief is possible even without intercession, so with intercession it is even more likely. Forgiveness of disbelief, while rationally possible, is prohibited by revelation, citing Quran 4:48. The commentator notes that 'permissibility' here includes both rational and scriptural. He then gives a wisdom: sinners fear punishment and hope for mercy because they believe their souls are deficient, unlike disbelievers. He criticizes this reasoning as incomplete because intercession is broader than just forgiveness of sins. Next, he explains the line 'So we do not excommunicate a believer for sin.' This is derived from the previous point. The ruling applies to any sin, minor or major, committed by a Muslim, with two conditions: the sin must not be an excommunicating matter (like denying God's knowledge of particulars), and the sinner must not deem it permissible (halal). If these conditions are violated, excommunication occurs. He then quotes Ibn Abidin regarding innovators like Mu'tazila and Rafidah: they are not excommunicated due to their interpretations. Finally, he contrasts the Khawarij and Mu'tazila: the Khawarij excommunicate all sinners and deem all sins major, while the Mu'tazila put the committer of a major sin in a middle position (al-manzilah bayn al-manzilatayn), neither believer nor disbeliever unless they deem it permissible. Both groups hold that such a person will be eternally in Hell.

Key Terms
غفران الذنوب
الشفاعة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is forgiveness of disbelief scripturally prohibited?
A) Because it is rationally impossible
B) Because the disbeliever lacks fear and hope
C) Because the Quran explicitly states that Allah does not forgive shirk
D) Because intercession is required

Key Takeaway
Forgiveness of sins other than disbelief is permissible both rationally and scripturally, establishing the basis for intercession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you truly fear Allah's punishment for your sins, or have you become indifferent because you assume forgiveness is easy? Ponder a specific sin you committed recently. Did it lead you to humility or arrogance? Commit to sincere repentance and renew your awareness of your need for mercy.
?
The disbeliever is described as having no hope for pardon because he does not believe his soul is deficient. In what areas of your life do you act as if you are self-sufficient, relying on your own abilities rather than depending on Allah? How can you cultivate a constant sense of spiritual poverty and need for Allah's forgiveness starting today?
?
You believe in intercession, but does that belief make you complacent in avoiding sin? The passage reminds us that forgiveness is for those who fear and hope. Are you truly among those who fear Allah's punishment and hope for His mercy, or do you take grace for granted? What concrete action will you take today to increase your consciousness of your sins and your longing for Allah's forgiveness?

The Rule of Not Excommunicating a Believer for Sin
Ruling on not excommunicating a believer for sin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that a believer is not excommunicated for committing a sin, unless the sin itself is an act of disbelief or the person deems it permissible.

Core Idea
A believer remains a believer even when sinning, unless the sin is excommunicating or deemed halal.

This passage addresses the critical issue of takfir. The commentator explains that a believer is not excommunicated for any sin, minor or major, with two exceptions: the sin must not be among the matters that constitute disbelief (like denying God's knowledge), and the person must not deem the sin permissible. The text also contrasts the positions of the Khawarij and Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two conditions for excommunication.
Conditions: sin is from mukaffirat OR deemed halal.
2
Understand the exceptions
What are the two cases where a believer is excommunicated? Why are they excepted?
3
Analyze the contrasts
Compare the positions of Khawarij (excommunicate all sinners) and Mu'tazila (middle position). How do they differ from Sunni view?
Khawarij: all sins major, excommunicate. Mu'tazila: no faith but not disbelief unless istihlal.
4
Reflect on the principle
Why is it important not to excommunicate a believer? Consider the implications for unity and mercy.

That is because the sinner is a Muslim who believes his soul is deficient, so he fears punishment and hopes for pardon and mercy, unlike the disbeliever who does not believe his soul is deficient, so he does not fear punishment nor hope for pardon and mercy. It is not hidden that this reasoning mentioned by the author is deficient because intercession is comprehensive in the chapter of judgment, and for intercession in forgiveness of sins, and this reasoning is specific to intercession in forgiveness of sins, so ponder it. His statement: 'So we do not excommunicate a believer for sin' is a branch of what was mentioned: i.e., we do not excommunicate, with the nun (i.e., we the community of Ahl al-Sunnah) or with the ta (i.e., you the addressee) anyone among the believers for committing a sin, whether the sin is minor or major, whether the committer is knowledgeable or ignorant, on condition that that sin is not among the excommunicating matters such as denying His knowledge of particulars, otherwise its committer is excommunicated absolutely, and on condition that he does not deem it permissible (2); that is known from the religion by necessity like adultery, otherwise he is excommunicated for deeming it permissible (3). Al-Allamah Ibn Abidin said in his gloss (11/5): 'You are well aware that the correct position regarding the Mu'tazila, the Rafidah, and other innovators is that they are not judged to be disbelievers even if they insult the Companions or deem it permissible to kill us due to a semblance of evidence, like the Khawarij who deemed it permissible to kill the Companions.' The Khawarij opposed and excommunicated the committer of sins and made all sins major, as will come, and they were not excommunicated for excommunicating the committer of sins, even though whoever excommunicates a believer becomes a disbeliever, because they said that with interpretation and ijtihad. As for the Mu'tazila, they removed the committer of a major sin from faith, and did not enter him into disbelief except with deemed permissibility, so they made it a station between two stations. Thus the committer of a major sin is eternally in Hell according to both groups, and is punished with the punishment of disbelievers according to the Khawarij, and with the punishment of sinners according to the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two lines of the poem. It begins by explaining the line 'Since it is permissible to forgive other than disbelief.' The commentator states that this is a justification for intercession (shafa'a): both reason and revelation show that forgiveness of sins other than disbelief is possible even without intercession, so with intercession it is even more likely. Forgiveness of disbelief, while rationally possible, is prohibited by revelation, citing Quran 4:48. The commentator notes that 'permissibility' here includes both rational and scriptural. He then gives a wisdom: sinners fear punishment and hope for mercy because they believe their souls are deficient, unlike disbelievers. He criticizes this reasoning as incomplete because intercession is broader than just forgiveness of sins. Next, he explains the line 'So we do not excommunicate a believer for sin.' This is derived from the previous point. The ruling applies to any sin, minor or major, committed by a Muslim, with two conditions: the sin must not be an excommunicating matter (like denying God's knowledge of particulars), and the sinner must not deem it permissible (halal). If these conditions are violated, excommunication occurs. He then quotes Ibn Abidin regarding innovators like Mu'tazila and Rafidah: they are not excommunicated due to their interpretations. Finally, he contrasts the Khawarij and Mu'tazila: the Khawarij excommunicate all sinners and deem all sins major, while the Mu'tazila put the committer of a major sin in a middle position (al-manzilah bayn al-manzilatayn), neither believer nor disbeliever unless they deem it permissible. Both groups hold that such a person will be eternally in Hell.

Key Terms
تكفير المؤمن
الكبائر
المعتزلة
الخوارج
منزلة بين المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any sin automatically makes a Muslim a disbeliever or excommunicated.
Correction: The text clarifies that only sins that are excommunicating matters or done with istihlal cause excommunication; otherwise, the person remains a believer.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition does a Muslim become excommunicated for a sin?
A) If the sin is a major sin
B) If the sin is an excommunicating matter or if he deems it permissible
C) If he does not repent before death
D) If he is a common person

Key Takeaway
A believer is not excommunicated for sin unless the sin itself is an act of disbelief or the person deems it permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever silently judged another Muslim's faith based on a sin they committed, perhaps even considering them 'not a true believer'? This lesson says that only two conditions warrant excommunication. Will you now examine your heart and repent from any unjust takfir you have made?
?
The lesson warns that deeming a sin permissible leads to disbelief. Can you think of any sin you have rationalized or made excuses for, treating it as acceptable? How will you realign your conscience with the clear rulings of the religion?
?
The Khawarij's harshness led them to excommunicate sinners and even kill Companions. Do you see any trace of that harshness in your own attitude toward Muslims who fall into sin? What concrete step will you take today to replace that harshness with mercy and correct understanding?

The Fate of a Believer Who Dies Without Repentance
Explanation of the line with grammar, theology, and proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a believer who dies without repenting from a major sin is not subject to eternal punishment, and his ultimate destiny is left to God's will, with the certainty that he will eventually enter Paradise.

Core Idea
A Muslim's final destination is Paradise, even if he dies with unrepented major sins.

This lesson delves into a crucial theological principle from the poem: the fate of a believer who dies without repentance. The commentator explains the grammatical structure of the line and then clarifies the doctrinal stance—neither presuming forgiveness nor guaranteeing punishment, but affirming that any punishment will be temporary, not eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the complete Arabic text and its literal translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Understand the grammatical breakdown
Notice how the conditional sentence structure supports the theological nuance: the protasis includes the state of not repenting, and the apodosis entrusts the matter to God.
3
Grasp the theological ruling
Focus on the middle section where the commentator says we do not decide on forgiveness or punishment, but that punishment is not eternal.
4
Examine the proofs
Look at the Quranic verse (99:7) and hadith cited, and the logical argument that Paradise is eternal, so the believer must enter without prior entrance into Hell.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this position balances between despair (eternal punishment) and presumption (automatic forgiveness), teaching hope and accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١١٦ - وَمَنْ يَمُتْ وَلَمْ يَتُبْ مِنْ ذَنْبِهِ فَأَمْرُهُ مُفَوَّضٌ لِرَبِّهِ. قوله : (وَمَنْ) اسم شرط جازم مبتدأ. و(يَمُتْ) فعل الشرط مجزوم بالسكون، وجمله فعل الشرط في محل رفع خبر المبتدأ على الراجح. و (لَمْ يَتُبْ مِنْ ذَنْبِهِ) جملة حالية مرتبطة بالواو. وجملة (فَأَمْرُهُ مُفَوَّضٌ لِرَبِّهِ) في محل جزم جواب الشرط؛ أي: ومن يمت بعد أن ارتكب ذنباً من الكبائر غير المكفرة بلا استحلال والحال أنه لم يتب من ذنبه إلى الله تعالى، فأمره وشأنه مفوض وموكول إلى ربه، فلا نقطع بالعفو عنه؛ لئلا تكون الذنوب في حكم المباحة، ولا بالعقوبة؛ لأنه تعالى يجوز عليه أن يغفر ما عدا الكفر؛ وعلى تقدير وقوع العقاب نقطع له بعدم الخلود في النار، كما أشار إليه بقوله الآتي: (ثم الخلود مجتنب)، وهذا هو مذهب أهل الحق، واستدلوا عليه بالآيات والأحاديث الدالة على أن المؤمنين يدخلون الجنة البتة : كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ﴾ [الزلزلة : ٧] . وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: مَنْ قال لا إله إلا الله دخل الجنة، ولا يصح أنه يدخل الجنة ثم يدخل النار؛ لأن من دخل الجنة لا يخرج منها ، قال تعالى: ﴿وَمَا هُم مِّنْهَا بِمُخْرَجِينَ﴾ [الحجر: ٤٨]، فتعين أن يكون دخوله الجنة بدون دخول النار بالمرة.
116 - And whoever dies and does not repent from his sin, then his affair is entrusted to his Lord. His statement: (And whoever) is a conditional noun, in the nominative as subject. (He dies) is the verb of the condition, in the jussive with sukun, and the clause of the verb of the condition is in the nominative as predicate of the subject according to the preponderant view. And (he did not repent from his sin) is a circumstantial clause linked by the waw. And the clause (then his affair is entrusted to his Lord) is in the jussive as the response of the condition; i.e., whoever dies after having committed a major sin that is not excommunicating and without deeming it permissible, while in the state that he did not repent from his sin to Allah, then his affair and matter are entrusted and delegated to his Lord. So we do not decide on forgiveness for him, lest sins become in the ruling of permissible things, nor on punishment, because it is permissible for Him to forgive what is other than disbelief. And on the assumption of the occurrence of punishment, we decide for him that there is no eternal abiding in the Fire, as he indicated by his subsequent statement: (Then eternal abiding is avoided). This is the doctrine of the people of truth, and they argued for it with verses and hadiths indicating that believers will definitely enter Paradise, such as His statement: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' [Az-Zalzala 7], and his statement, peace be upon him: 'Whoever says there is no god but Allah will enter Paradise.' And it is not valid that he enters Paradise then enters the Fire, because whoever enters Paradise does not leave it. Allah said: 'And they will not be removed from it' [Al-Hijr 48]. So it is determined that his entering Paradise is without entering the Fire at all.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological explanation of the line 'And whoever dies and does not repent from his sin, then his affair is entrusted to his Lord.' The commentator begins with a grammatical breakdown: 'Whoever' is a conditional noun, 'dies' is the verb of the condition, 'he did not repent' is a circumstantial clause, and the response is 'his affair is entrusted to his Lord.' The meaning is that a believer who commits a major non-excommunicating sin without deeming it permissible and dies without repentance, his ultimate fate is left to God's will. We do not assert that he will be forgiven (lest sins seem trivial) nor that he will be punished (since God may forgive). However, if punishment occurs, it is not eternal; the believer will eventually enter Paradise, based on the subsequent line 'then eternal abiding is avoided.' The commentator then provides evidence: Quran 99:7 and the hadith 'Whoever says la ilaha illa Allah will enter Paradise.' He argues that entering Paradise then entering Hell is impossible because Paradise is eternal, citing Quran 15:48. Thus, such a believer must enter Paradise without ever entering Hell, meaning any punishment is temporary. This is the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth).

Key Terms
تفويض الأمر إلى الله
عدم الخلود
الذنوب غير المكفرة
أهل الحق
دخول الجنة بلا دخول النار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any sin, even from a believer, leads to eternal punishment in Hell.
Correction: The text teaches that a believer's ultimate destiny is Paradise; any punishment is temporary and non-eternal, and his final outcome is guaranteed entrance into Paradise.

Checkpoint

B) He will definitely be punished eternally
C) His affair is left to God, but if punished, it will not be eternal
D) He is considered a disbeliever

Key Takeaway
The fate of a Muslim who dies with unrepented major sins is left to God's will, but he will ultimately enter Paradise without eternal punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson warns that we cannot decide for forgiveness, so ask yourself: Is your confidence in God's mercy leading you to take your sins lightly? What will you do today to turn to Him in repentance before it is too late?
?
If you died right now with unrepented sins, do you truly trust that God will deal with you justly and mercifully? Or are you secretly afraid that your sins are too great? This lesson assures that a believer's final destination is Paradise, but temporary punishment is possible. How does that affect your urgency in seeking forgiveness? Are you content to leave your affair to God without active repentance?
?

The Fate of the Unrepentant Sinner
Grammatical and theological explanation of verse 116.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni theological position on the fate of the unrepentant sinner and the grammatical structure of the conditional sentence in verse 116.

Core Idea
A grave sinner's fate is entrusted to Allah; neither guaranteed forgiveness nor eternal punishment.

This lesson unpacks verse 116 of the Jawharat al-Tawhid. It begins with a grammatical analysis of the conditional sentence, then draws the theological conclusion: the unrepentant grave sinner's affair is with Allah. We neither promise forgiveness nor threaten eternal punishment, but affirm that any punishment will be temporary. This is the Ash'ari position, supported by explicit Quranic verses and hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the conditional structure starting with 'Wa man yamut'.
2
Understand the grammatical breakdown
Follow the commentary's parsing: identify 'man' as conditional noun, 'yamut' as verb of condition, and the circumstantial clause 'lam yatub'. See how the response 'fa amruhu mufawwad' is in the jussive.
The grammar underpins the theological point of entrusting.
3
Grasp the theological meaning
The author explains that we do not decide on forgiveness nor punishment. The matter is delegated to Allah. However, if punishment occurs, it is not eternal. This is the 'madhhab ahl al-haqq'.
This position avoids both despair and presumption.
4
Review the proofs cited
The author quotes Quran 99:7 and the hadith 'Whoever says La ilaha illa Allah will enter Paradise' to show believers eventually enter Paradise, and once there, they never leave. This supports temporary punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١١٦ - وَمَنْ يَمُتْ ولَمْ يَتُبْ مِنْ ذَنْبِهِ فَأَمْرُهُ مُفَوَّضٌ لِرَبِّهِ قوله : (وَمَنْ) اسمُ شرط جازم مبتدأ. و(يَمُتْ) فعل الشرط مجزوم بالسكون، وجمله فعل الشرط في محل رفع خبر المبتدأ على الراجح. و (لَمْ يَتُبْ مِنْ ذَنْبِهِ) جملةٌ حالية مرتبطة بالواو . وجملة (فَأَمْرُهُ مُفَوَّضٌ لِرَبِّهِ) في محل جزم جواب الشرط؛ أي: ومن يمت بعد أن ارتكب ذنباً من الكبائر غير المكفرة بلا استحلال والحال أنه لم يتب من ذنبه إلى الله تعالى، فأمره وشأنه مُفوَّضٌ وموكول إلى ربِّه، فلا نقطع بالعفو عنه؛ لئلا تكون الذنوب في حكم المباحة، ولا بالعقوبة؛ لأنه تعالى يجوز عليه أن يغفر ما عدا الكفر؛ وعلى تقدير وقوع العقاب نقطع له بعدم الخلود في النار، كما أشار إليه بقوله الآتي: (ثم الخلود مجتنب)، وهذا هو مذهب أهل الحقِّ، واستدلوا عليه بالآيات والأحاديث الدالة على أن المؤمنين يدخلون الجنة البتة : كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ ﴾ [الزلزلة : ٧] . وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: مَنْ قال لا إله إلا الله دخل الجنةَ ) ، ولا يصح أنه يدخل الجنة ثم يدخل النار ؛ لأن مَنْ دخل الجنة لا يخرج منها ، قال تعالى: ﴿وَمَا هُم مِّنْهَا بِمُخْرَجِينَ ﴾ [الحجر: ٤٨]، فتعين أن يكون دخوله الجنة بدون دخول النار بالمرة، وهذا هو العفو التام، أو بعد دخول النار بقَدْرِ ذنبه ، وهذا هو عدم الخلود في النار )
116. And whoever dies and does not repent from his sin, his affair is entrusted to his Lord. His saying: (Wa man) is a conditional noun in the nominative as subject. (yamut) is the verb of the condition in jussive with sukun, and the sentence of the condition is in the nominative as predicate of the subject according to the most correct view. And (lam yatub min dhanbihi) is a circumstantial clause connected with waw. And the sentence (fa amruhu mufawwad li-rabbihi) is in the jussive as the response of the condition. Meaning: Whoever dies after committing a major sin that is not expiated without deeming it permissible, while he has not repented from his sin to Allah, then his affair and matter are entrusted and delegated to his Lord. So we do not decide for sure about forgiveness to avoid sins being treated as permissible, nor about punishment because He may forgive anything other than disbelief. And on the assumption of punishment, we decide for sure that he will not be immortal in the Fire, as he will indicate in his coming saying: (thumma al-khulud mujtanab). This is the madhhab of the people of truth. They supported it with verses and hadiths indicating that believers will enter Paradise absolutely, such as His saying: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' [Zalzala:7], and his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever says La ilaha illa Allah will enter Paradise.' And it is not valid that he enters Paradise then enters the Fire, because whoever enters Paradise does not leave it, as He said: 'And they will not be brought out of it' [Hijr:48]. So it is determined that his entry into Paradise is either without entering the Fire at all—this is complete forgiveness—or after entering the Fire for the amount of his sin—this is the absence of immortality in the Fire.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological explanation of verse 116. It first parses the conditional sentence grammatically. Then it explains the meaning: the one who dies with a major sin without repentance is left to Allah's will. The author emphasizes that we cannot guarantee forgiveness (to avoid taking sins lightly) nor guarantee punishment (because Allah may forgive anything except disbelief). However, if punishment does occur, it is not eternal. This is the Ash'ari position. Proofs are cited: Quran and hadith that believers will eventually enter Paradise, and once in Paradise they never leave. So the grave sinner either enters Paradise directly (if forgiven) or after temporary punishment in Hell.

Key Terms
الشرط
الجواب
التفويض
العفو
الخلود
مذهب أهل الحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Sunni view guarantees forgiveness for any grave sinner.
Correction: The passage explicitly says we do not decide for sure about forgiveness; the matter is entrusted to Allah. We only affirm that if punished, it is not eternal.

Key Takeaway
The fate of the unrepentant grave sinner is left to Allah's will; we neither guarantee forgiveness nor eternal punishment, but affirm temporary punishment if incurred.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Obligatory Punishment for Major Sinners
The author explains that punishment of some major sinners is obligatory by divine decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine decree includes punishment for some unrepentant major sinners from this community, and the exceptions of those with valid interpretation or repentance.

Core Idea
Punishment for sin is established by divine decree, not coercion.

This lesson examines verse 117, where the author clarifies that punishment for certain sinners is obligatory in the sense of being decreed by Allah. We will unpack the grammatical structure and the conditions that exempt sinners from this punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and grammar
Begin by reading the Arabic verse and its grammatical breakdown. Note how 'wajib' functions as predicate.
2
Identify the ruling
The author states that punishment of some sinners from this community is obligatory. Understand that 'obligatory' here means established by sacred law, not that Allah is forced.
3
Recognize the exceptions
Two groups are exempt: those who commit minor sins, those who commit major sins with valid interpretation, and those who repent before death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١١٧ - وَوَاجِبٌ تَعْذِيبُ بَعْضِ ارْتَكَبْ كَبِيرَةً ثُمَّ الخُلُوْدُ مُجْتَنَبْ قوله : (وَوَاجِبٌ تَعْذِيبُ بَعْضِ ارْتَكَبُ ، كَبِيْرَةٌ) (واجب) خبر مقدم، و(تعذيب) مبتدأ مؤخَّرٌ؛ أي: وتعذيب بعض غير معين من عصاة هذه الأمة ارتكب كبيرة من غير تأويل يُعذر به ومات بلا توبة واجبٌ؛ أي ثابت وواقع شرعاً، بخلاف من ارتكب صغيرة أو ارتكب كبيرة بتأويل، كما يقع من البغاة المتأوّلين، أو ارتكبها من غير تأويل، لكن مات بعد التوبة . وهل المراد بهذه الأمة : أمة الدعوة: فتشمل الكفار، فيجوز أن يكون البعض المعذَّب على الكبائر غير الكفر بعض الكفار ؛ وعلى هذا يجوز طلب المغفرة لجميع المسلمين . أو أمه الإجابة: فلا تشمل الكفار ، فلا يجوز أن يكون البعض المعذَّب على الكبائر بعض الكفار، بل لا بد أن يكون من المسلمين؟ :قولان جرى الشيخ عبد السلام على الأوَّل، والمعتمد الثاني، والمراد بالبعض المذكور طائفةٌ، ولو واحداً من كل صنف من العصاة كالزناة وقَتَلَة النفس وشَرَبَة الخمر وهكذا، فلا بدَّ من نفوذ الوعيد في طائفة من كلِّ صنفٍ أقلها واحد. لكن هذه المسألة مبنية على طريقة الماتريدية من أنه لا أنه لا يجوز تخلف الوعيد
117. And obligatory is the punishment of some who committed a major sin, then eternal punishment is avoided. His saying: (Wa wajibun ta'dhibu ba'di irtakaba kabiratan) (Wajib) is a predicate in the nominative and (ta'dhib) is a delayed subject. Meaning: And the punishment of an unspecified part of the sinners of this community who committed a major sin without a valid excuse and died without repentance is obligatory, i.e., established and occurring by sacred law, unlike the one who committed a minor sin or committed a major sin with ta'wil, as occurs from the rebellious who interpret, or committed it without ta'wil but died after repentance. And is the intention by 'this community' the community of invitation (ummat al-da'wa), which includes the disbelievers, so it is possible that the specific part punished for major sins other than disbelief is some disbelievers; and on this basis it is permissible to seek forgiveness for all Muslims. Or the community of response (ummat al-ijaba), which does not include the disbelievers, so it is not possible that the specific part punished for major sins is some disbelievers, but rather it must be from among the Muslims? There are two opinions: Shaykh Abd al-Salam followed the first, and the relied-upon is the second. And the intended by 'the specific part' is a group, even one, from every type of sinners, such as adulterers, murderers, wine drinkers, and so on. So it is necessary that the threat be executed upon a group from each type, at least one. But this issue is based on the Maturidi method that it is not permissible for the threat to be unfulfilled.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage explains verse 117. It begins with grammatical analysis. Then it states that punishment of some unspecified sinners from this community who die without repentance is obligatory (wajib) in the sense of being established by divine decree. Exceptions are those with valid interpretation or who repent. Then it discusses two interpretations of 'this community': ummat al-da'wa (all invited, including disbelievers) and ummat al-ijaba (only believers). Sheikh Abd al-Salam chose the first, but the relied-upon is the second. The 'some' that are punished must include at least one from each category of sinners. The author notes that this issue is based on the Maturidi position that the threat (wa'id) must be executed, otherwise God's threat would be false. This differs from the Ash'ari view that God may forgive without punishment.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'obligatory' means Allah is compelled to punish.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'wajib' here means established and occurring by sacred law, not that Allah is under compulsion.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'wajib' (obligatory) mean in the context of punishment?
A) It is obligatory upon Allah to punish.
B) It is established and occurring by divine decree.
C) It is recommended but not certain.

Key Takeaway
Punishment of unrepentant major sinners is established by divine decree, with exceptions for minor sins, valid interpretation, and repentance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The punishment of some sinners is 'obligatory' by divine decree—this is not a threat but a reality of divine justice. Does this thought make you tremble before Allah? How will you change your relationship with sin starting this moment?

Interpreting 'This Community' and the Maturidi Threat
The author discusses two interpretations of 'this community' and the Maturidi view on threat fulfillment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of 'this community' in verse 117 and the Maturidi basis for the necessity of executing the threat.

Core Idea
The threat of punishment must be fulfilled for at least one from each type of sinner.

In this part of the commentary, the author addresses a key theological question: does 'this community' include disbelievers or only believers? The answer impacts our understanding of divine punishment. We also learn that the Maturidi school holds that God's threat must be executed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
Read the passage and note the two interpretations of 'this community': ummat al-da'wa (including disbelievers) and ummat al-ijaba (only believers).
2
Understand the implications
The first opinion allows seeking forgiveness for all Muslims; the second restricts punishment to Muslims.
3
Grasp the Maturidi foundation
The author explains that the issue is based on the Maturidi principle that the threat (wa'id) cannot be unfulfilled.

١١٧ - وَوَاجِبٌ تَعْذِيبُ بَعْضِ ارْتَكَبْ كَبِيرَةً ثُمَّ الخُلُوْدُ مُجْتَنَبْ قوله : (وَوَاجِبٌ تَعْذِيبُ بَعْضِ ارْتَكَبُ ، كَبِيْرَةٌ) (واجب) خبر مقدم، و(تعذيب) مبتدأ مؤخَّرٌ؛ أي: وتعذيب بعض غير معين من عصاة هذه الأمة ارتكب كبيرة من غير تأويل يُعذر به ومات بلا توبة واجبٌ؛ أي ثابت وواقع شرعاً، بخلاف من ارتكب صغيرة أو ارتكب كبيرة بتأويل، كما يقع من البغاة المتأوّلين، أو ارتكبها من غير تأويل، لكن مات بعد التوبة . وهل المراد بهذه الأمة : أمة الدعوة: فتشمل الكفار، فيجوز أن يكون البعض المعذَّب على الكبائر غير الكفر بعض الكفار ؛ وعلى هذا يجوز طلب المغفرة لجميع المسلمين . أو أمه الإجابة: فلا تشمل الكفار ، فلا يجوز أن يكون البعض المعذَّب على الكبائر بعض الكفار، بل لا بد أن يكون من المسلمين؟ :قولان جرى الشيخ عبد السلام على الأوَّل، والمعتمد الثاني، والمراد بالبعض المذكور طائفةٌ، ولو واحداً من كل صنف من العصاة كالزناة وقَتَلَة النفس وشَرَبَة الخمر وهكذا، فلا بدَّ من نفوذ الوعيد في طائفة من كلِّ صنفٍ أقلها واحد. لكن هذه المسألة مبنية على طريقة الماتريدية من أنه لا أنه لا يجوز تخلف الوعيد
117. And obligatory is the punishment of some who committed a major sin, then eternal punishment is avoided. His saying: (Wa wajibun ta'dhibu ba'di irtakaba kabiratan) (Wajib) is a predicate in the nominative and (ta'dhib) is a delayed subject. Meaning: And the punishment of an unspecified part of the sinners of this community who committed a major sin without a valid excuse and died without repentance is obligatory, i.e., established and occurring by sacred law, unlike the one who committed a minor sin or committed a major sin with ta'wil, as occurs from the rebellious who interpret, or committed it without ta'wil but died after repentance. And is the intention by 'this community' the community of invitation (ummat al-da'wa), which includes the disbelievers, so it is possible that the specific part punished for major sins other than disbelief is some disbelievers; and on this basis it is permissible to seek forgiveness for all Muslims. Or the community of response (ummat al-ijaba), which does not include the disbelievers, so it is not possible that the specific part punished for major sins is some disbelievers, but rather it must be from among the Muslims? There are two opinions: Shaykh Abd al-Salam followed the first, and the relied-upon is the second. And the intended by 'the specific part' is a group, even one, from every type of sinners, such as adulterers, murderers, wine drinkers, and so on. So it is necessary that the threat be executed upon a group from each type, at least one. But this issue is based on the Maturidi method that it is not permissible for the threat to be unfulfilled.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage explains verse 117. It begins with grammatical analysis. Then it states that punishment of some unspecified sinners from this community who die without repentance is obligatory (wajib) in the sense of being established by divine decree. Exceptions are those with valid interpretation or who repent. Then it discusses two interpretations of 'this community': ummat al-da'wa (all invited, including disbelievers) and ummat al-ijaba (only believers). Sheikh Abd al-Salam chose the first, but the relied-upon is the second. The 'some' that are punished must include at least one from each category of sinners. The author notes that this issue is based on the Maturidi position that the threat (wa'id) must be executed, otherwise God's threat would be false. This differs from the Ash'ari view that God may forgive without punishment.

الوعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Maturidi view forces God to punish all sinners.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the threat must be executed on at least one from each category, not all individuals.

Checkpoint
Which interpretation of 'this community' is the relied-upon (mu'tamad) according to the passage?
A) Ummat al-Da'wa (community of invitation)
B) Ummat al-Ijaba (community of response)
C) Both are equally valid.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon view is that 'this community' refers to believers only, and the threat of punishment must be fulfilled for at least one from each category of sin, based on Maturidi theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God’s threat of punishment must be fulfilled for at least one from each type of sinner, do you honestly consider yourself among those who might be that one? What sin are you most afraid of facing punishment for, and what are you doing about it right now?
?
The relied-upon view says the community punished is believers only. How does that change your sense of personal accountability for your own major sins? Are you living as if you truly believe that even one believer could be singled out for punishment?
?
The Maturidi principle ensures God’s word is truthful. If you truly believed that every divine threat will come to pass, what would you change today about how you view your sins or seek repentance?

Obligation to Punish Some Grave Sinners
Commentary on obligation to punish some grave sinners with exceptions and community debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions under which punishment for major sins is obligatory according to Sunni theology, including the exceptions for minor sins, valid interpretations, and repentance, as well as the two interpretations of 'this community'.

Core Idea
Punishment for major sins is obligatory for some, but not all, based on key exceptions.

This lesson examines the first part of the commentary on line 117 of the Jawharah. It clarifies who exactly is subject to the divine threat of punishment for major sins, distinguishing between those with valid excuses and those without. The discussion also introduces a critical debate about whether 'this community' refers to all of humanity or only believers, shaping how we understand God's justice and mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once to grasp the main claim about obligatory punishment.
Pay attention to the grammatical parsing at the start.
2
Identify the three exceptions
Look for the exceptions mentioned: minor sins, major sins with valid interpretation, and repentance before death.
3
Understand the two interpretations of 'this community'
Notice the debate between Ummah al-Da'wah and Ummah al-Ijabah, and which opinion is relied upon.
The relied-upon view is that the community refers to believers only.
4
Reflect on the requirement of punishment for each sin category
The passage states that at least one sinner from each category must be punished. Consider why this is significant.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١١٧ - وَوَاجِبٌ تَعْذِيبُ بَعْضِ ارْتَكَبْ كَبِيرَةً ثُمَّ الْخُلُوْدُ مُجْتَنَبْ قوله : (وَوَاجِبٌ تَعْذِيبُ بَعْضِ ارْتَكَبُ كَبِيْرَةٌ) (واجب) خبر مقدم، و(تعذيب) مبتدأ مؤخَّرٌ؛ أي: وتعذيب بعض غير معين من عصاة هذه الأمة ارتكب كبيرة من غير تأويل يُعذر به ومات بلا توبة واجبٌ؛ أي ثابت وواقع شرعاً، بخلاف من ارتكب صغيرة أو ارتكب كبيرة بتأويل، كما يقع من البغاة المتأوّلين، أو ارتكبها من غير تأويل، لكن مات بعد التوبة . وهل المراد بهذه الأمة : أمة الدعوة: فتشمل الكفار، فيجوز أن يكون البعض المعذَّب على الكبائر غير الكفر بعض الكفار ؛ وعلى هذا يجوز طلب المغفرة لجميع المسلمين . أو أمة الإجابة: فلا تشمل الكفار ، فلا يجوز أن يكون البعض المعذَّب على الكبائر بعض الكفار، بل لا بد أن يكون من المسلمين؟ :قولان جرى الشيخ عبد السلام على الأوَّل، والمعتمد الثاني، والمراد بالبعض المذكور طائفةٌ، ولو واحداً من كل صنف من العصاة كالزناة وقَتَلَة النفس وشَرَبَة الخمر وهكذا، فلا بدَّ من نفوذ الوعيد في طائفة من كلِّ صنفٍ أقلها واحد. لكن هذه المسألة مبنية على طريقة الماتريدية من أنه لا يجوز تخلف الوعيد وأما على طريقة الأشاعرة من أنه يجوز تخلف الوعيد؛ لأنه على تقدير المشيئة، كما هو عادة الكريم، فإنه إذا قال : إذا فعل زيد كذا أعاقبه . . كان المراد أعاقبه إن شئت، فلا يجب تعذيب بعض العصاة؛ لجواز تخلف الوعيد، نعم قد ورد تعذيب بعض الموحدين والشفاعة فيهم، لكن لا يعمُّ الأنواع كلَّها . قوله : (ثُمَّ الخُلُوْدُ مُجْتَنَبْ) أي: ثم خلود من أراد الله تعذيبه من عصاة المؤمنين مُجْتَنَبٌ وقوعه، فلا نقول به . والحاصل أن الناس على قسمين : مؤمنٌ ، وكافر؛ فالكافر مخلد في النار إجماعاً، والمؤمن على قسمين: طائع وعاص ؛ فالطائع في الجنة إجماعاً، والعاصي على قسمين تائب، وغيرُ تائب؛ فالتائب في الجنة إجماعاً وغير التائب في المشيئة، وعلى تقدير عذابِهِ لا يُخلَّد في النار.
117 - And it is obligatory to punish some who commit a major sin, then eternity (in hell) is avoided. His saying: 'And obligatory to punish some who committed a major sin' – (واجب) is a predicate fronted, (تعذيب) is a subject delayed; i.e., and punishing some unspecified among the sinners of this community who committed a major sin without a valid interpretation and died without repentance is obligatory; i.e., established and occurring legally, contrary to one who committed a minor sin or committed a major sin with an interpretation, as occurs from the misguided rebels, or committed it without interpretation but died after repentance. And is the intended meaning of 'this community' the community of invitation: so it includes the disbelievers, so it is permissible that the punished ones for major sins other than disbelief are some disbelievers; and on this it is permissible to seek forgiveness for all Muslims. Or the community of response: so it does not include the disbelievers, so it is not permissible that the punished ones for major sins are some disbelievers, rather it must be from the Muslims? Two opinions: Shaykh 'Abd al-Salam held the first, and the relied-upon is the second, and the intended by the mentioned 'some' is a group, even if one from each category of sinners like adulterers, murderers, wine-drinkers, and so on, so the threat must be fulfilled in a group from each category, the least of which is one. But this issue is based on the Maturidi way that it is not permissible for the threat to be unfulfilled. As for the Ash'ari way, it is permissible for the threat to be unfulfilled because it is contingent on will, as is the habit of the Generous, for if He says: 'If Zayd does such and such, I will punish him,' the intended is 'I will punish him if I will,' so it is not obligatory to punish some sinners due to the permissibility of unfulfillment of the threat. Yes, punishment of some monotheists and intercession for them has been reported, but it does not encompass all categories. His saying: 'Then eternity is avoided' i.e., then the eternity of those whom Allah wills to punish from the sinful believers is avoided in occurrence, so we do not say it. The summary is that people are of two types: believer and disbeliever; the disbeliever is eternal in hell by consensus. The believer is of two types: obedient and disobedient; the obedient is in paradise by consensus; the disobedient is of two types: repentant and unrepentant; the repentant is in paradise by consensus, and the unrepentant is subject to will, and if he is punished he is not eternal in hell.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on line 117 of the poem. It explains the obligation to punish some grave sinners and affirms that believing sinners will not be eternally in hell. The author first parses the grammar: 'واجب' is a fronted predicate, 'تعذيب' is a delayed subject. The meaning: it is obligatory (established legally) to punish some unspecified sinners from this community who died without repentance and without a valid excuse for their sin. Exceptions are those who committed minor sins or major sins with a valid interpretation (like misguided rebels), or repented before death. Then the author discusses which community is meant: 'Ummah al-Da'wah' (including disbelievers) or 'Ummah al-Ijabah' (only Muslims). He reports two opinions, preferring the second (only Muslims). The 'some' who are punished must include at least one from each category of sinners (e.g., adulterers, murderers, drunkards). The author then explains that this issue depends on the theological school: Maturidis hold that threats (وعيد) must be fulfilled, so punishment is obligatory for some. Ash'aris hold that threats are contingent on God's will, so punishment is not obligatory; indeed, God may forgive. Finally, the author summarizes the eschatological categories: disbelievers are eternal in hell; obedient believers and repentant sinners are in paradise; unrepentant sinners are subject to God's will and if punished are not eternal.

Key Terms
تعذيب
كبيرة
تأويل
أمة الدعوة
أمة الإجابة
وعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that all sinners will necessarily be punished or that repentance is irrelevant.
Correction: The passage clarifies that punishment is obligatory only for some, with clear exceptions including repentance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an exception to obligatory punishment?
A) Committing a major sin
B) Committing a major sin with a valid interpretation
C) Dying without repentance
D) Being a disbeliever

Key Takeaway
Punishment for major sins is obligatory only for a subset of unrepentant sinners without valid excuse, and it must affect at least one from each sin category among the Muslim community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you currently holding onto a major sin without repentance, assuming God's mercy will automatically excuse you? What would it mean for you if you died tonight as one of the 'ba'd' for whom punishment is obligatory?
?
The passage says punishment must affect at least one person from your category of sin. Does that thought make you tremble or do you feel immune? How does this affect your urgency to repent before death?
?
If you have used a personal 'ta'wil' to justify a sin, how can you be certain it is a valid excuse and not self-deception? What would it take for you to abandon that excuse and turn to God?

Maturidi and Ash'ari Views on Divine Threats and the Fate of Sinful Believers
Commentary on theological basis for punishment and summary of eschatological categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between Maturidi and Ash'ari positions on whether God's threats of punishment must be fulfilled, and how this affects the eternal fate of sinful believers.

Core Idea
Maturidis say threats must be fulfilled; Ash'aris say they may be forgiven by God's will.

This lesson continues the commentary on line 117, shifting from the identity of punished sinners to the theological basis for punishment. The author explains how the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools diverge on whether God's threats (وعيد) are absolute or contingent on His will. The passage concludes with a clear summary of eschatological categories, affirming that no believing sinner will remain in hell eternally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Maturidi and Ash'ari views
Pay attention to the key phrase: Maturidis hold that threats cannot be unfulfilled, while Ash'aris hold they can.
The example of a master's statement illustrates the Ash'ari position.
2
Understand the consequence for punishment
For Maturidis, punishment must occur for some; for Ash'aris, it is not obligatory because God may forgive.
3
Analyze the final summary of categories
The passage divides people into believer/disbeliever and then obedient/disobedient/repentant, concluding with the crucial point that punished believers are not eternal in hell.
Note the consensus on eternity of disbelievers and paradise for obedient and repentant.
4
Connect to the earlier lesson
See how the Maturidi/Ash'ari difference underlies the earlier discussion of the necessity of punishment for some.

His saying: 'Then eternity is avoided' i.e., then the eternity of those whom Allah wills to punish from the sinful believers is avoided in occurrence, so we do not say it. The summary is that people are of two types: believer and disbeliever; the disbeliever is eternal in hell by consensus. The believer is of two types: obedient and disobedient; the obedient is in paradise by consensus; the disobedient is of two types: repentant and unrepentant; the repentant is in paradise by consensus, and the unrepentant is subject to will, and if he is punished he is not eternal in hell.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on line 117 of the poem. It explains the obligation to punish some grave sinners and affirms that believing sinners will not be eternally in hell. The author first parses the grammar: 'واجب' is a fronted predicate, 'تعذيب' is a delayed subject. The meaning: it is obligatory (established legally) to punish some unspecified sinners from this community who died without repentance and without a valid excuse for their sin. Exceptions are those who committed minor sins or major sins with a valid interpretation (like misguided rebels), or repented before death. Then the author discusses which community is meant: 'Ummah al-Da'wah' (including disbelievers) or 'Ummah al-Ijabah' (only Muslims). He reports two opinions, preferring the second (only Muslims). The 'some' who are punished must include at least one from each category of sinners (e.g., adulterers, murderers, drunkards). The author then explains that this issue depends on the theological school: Maturidis hold that threats (وعيد) must be fulfilled, so punishment is obligatory for some. Ash'aris hold that threats are contingent on God's will, so punishment is not obligatory; indeed, God may forgive. Finally, the author summarizes the eschatological categories: disbelievers are eternal in hell; obedient believers and repentant sinners are in paradise; unrepentant sinners are subject to God's will and if punished are not eternal.

Key Terms
وعيد
ماتريدية
أشاعرة
خلود
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that believing sinners can be eternally in hell or that God is unjust if He forgives without punishment.
Correction: The passage affirms that no believing sinner is eternal in hell, and God's threats may be contingent on His will (Ash'ari) or must be fulfilled (Maturidi) but only for some.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position, why is punishment for some sinners not obligatory?
A) Because God cannot punish
B) Because threats are contingent on His will
C) Because all sinners repent
D) Because punishment is only for disbelievers

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi and Ash'ari schools differ on whether God's threats must be fulfilled, but both agree that believing sinners, if punished, are not eternal in hell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to die right now with unrepented sins, would you be more afraid of God's justice or hopeful of His mercy? How does this uncertainty about your own fate push you to change something today?
?
The Ash'ari view says God's threats are conditional (إن شئت). Does that make you feel complacent or does it drive you to seek forgiveness more urgently? Honestly, do you rely on that condition to delay repentance?
?
Imagine standing before God and being told that your sins could be punished, but that He might also forgive. What would you want to have done in this life to prepare for that moment? What concrete step will you take this week to increase your reliance on God's mercy while fearing His justice?

The Real Life of the Martyr in Battle
Commentary on line 118: affirming real life for martyrs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the martyr's life is real and complete, distinct from worldly life, and that their provision is for pleasure.

Core Idea
Martyrs live a real life of pleasure, not need.

This lesson examines a foundational passage from the commentary on the poem 'Jawharat al-Tawhid' concerning the life of the martyr in battle. The author clarifies that although we cannot perceive its modality, the martyr possesses a complete, real life. This life, shared by all dead in a basic sense, is more perfect for martyrs and most perfect for prophets. Importantly, the martyr's provision is not for sustenance but for delight.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation, noting the key claim about the martyr's life.
2
Identify the degrees of life
Notice how the author compares the life of martyrs to other dead and to prophets. All are alive, but with different degrees of perfection.
The life is 'complete' (كاملة) for martyrs, but its modality is unknown.
3
Understand the nature of real life
The author insists this life is real (حقيقة) and attaches to both essence and soul. Real does not mean identical to worldly life.
4
Examine the ruling on food and drink
The passage explains that martyrs eat and drink for pleasure (تلذذ), not out of need (احتياج). This distinguishes their afterlife provision.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it affirms a tangible reward while preserving the difference between worldly and afterlife existence.
This counters the notion that the martyr's life is merely spiritual or metaphorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١١٨ - وَصِفْ شَهِيْدَ الحَرْبِ بِالحَياةِ وَرَزْقُهُ مِنْ مُشْتَهَى الجَنَّاتِ قوله: (وَصِفْ شَهِيْدَ الحَرْبِ بالحَياةِ :أي: اعتقد وجوباً اتصاف شهيد الحرب بالحياة الكاملة، وإن كان كيفيتها غير معلومةٍ لنا، والموتى وإن كانوا كلهم أحياء لاتصال أرواحهم بأجسامهم، لكن الشهداء أكملُ حياةً من غيرهم، والأنبياء أكمل حياةً من الشهداء، وهي ثابتة للذات والروح جميعاً، فهي حياة حقيقة، ولا يلزم من كونها حقيقة أن تكون الأبدان معها، كما كانت في الدنيا من الاحتياج للطعام والشراب وغيرهما من صفات الأجسام التي نشاهدها في الدنيا، بل يكون لها حكم آخر فأكلهم وشربهم للتلذذ لا للاحتياج.
118 - And describe the martyr of battle with life and his provision from the delights of paradise. His saying: (And describe the martyr of battle with life): i.e., believe necessarily that the martyr of battle is characterized by complete life, although its modality is unknown to us. And the dead, even though all of them are alive due to the connection of their souls to their bodies, the martyrs are more perfect in life than others, and the prophets are more perfect in life than the martyrs. And it (life) is established for both the essence and the soul, so it is a real life. And it does not follow from it being real that the bodies are with it as they were in the world in terms of needing food, drink, and other attributes of bodies that we observe in the world. Rather, they have a different ruling: their eating and drinking is for pleasure, not for need.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on line 118 concerning the martyr of battle (شهيد الحرب). The author commands the reader to affirm that the martyr is alive. He explains that this life is 'complete life' (حياة كاملة), though its exact nature is unknown to us. The author then compares martyrs to other dead: all dead are alive in some sense because their souls remain connected to their bodies, but martyrs have a more perfect life. Prophets have an even more perfect life than martyrs. This life is attributed to both the essence (ذات) and the soul (روح), so it is a real life (حياة حقيقة). However, the author emphasizes that this real life does not entail the same bodily needs as worldly life, such as needing food, drink, etc. Instead, martyrs receive a different ruling (حكم آخر): they eat and drink for pleasure (تلذذ), not out of necessity (احتياج).

Correction: The passage affirms it is a real life (حقيقة), but with different characteristics than worldly life—their provision is for pleasure, not need.

A) Because they need nourishment

?
You read that martyrs eat and drink for pleasure, not need. In your daily life, how much of your eating and drinking is driven by genuine need versus chasing pleasure? What does this say about your priorities in this worldly life?
